## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |

| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |

| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

**Sourcegraph Repository:** `github.com/sg-evals/TypeScript--v5.7.2`

# Task: Security Review of TypeScript Type Narrowing

## Objective
Review the TypeScript compiler's type narrowing implementation for patterns that could lead to unsound type assertions, enabling developers to bypass type safety unknowingly.

## Steps
1. Find the type narrowing implementation in `src/compiler/checker.ts`
2. Identify the narrowing functions (narrowType, narrowTypeByGuard, narrowTypeByTypeof, etc.)
3. Analyze potential unsoundness in:
   - typeof narrowing with user-defined type guards
   - Discriminated union narrowing edge cases
   - Control flow analysis across function boundaries
   - Type assertion vs type narrowing interaction
4. Create `security_review.md` in `/workspace/` documenting:
   - Overview of the narrowing architecture (file paths and functions)
   - At least 3 patterns where narrowing could produce unsound types
   - TypeScript code examples demonstrating each pattern
   - Severity assessment for each finding
   - Recommendations for stricter narrowing

## Key Reference Files
- `src/compiler/checker.ts` — main type checker with narrowing
- `src/compiler/types.ts` — type system definitions
- `src/compiler/utilities.ts` — helper functions

## Success Criteria
- security_review.md exists
- Identifies specific narrowing functions
- Documents at least 3 unsound patterns
- Includes TypeScript code examples

The body of the function is completely ignored from a safety perspective. The type guard is trusted unconditionally.\n\n```typescript\n// This type guard claims to check for numbers, but actually checks for strings\nfunction isNumber(x: string | number): x is number {\n    return typeof x === 'string'; // WRONG but TypeScript doesn't check body\n}\n\nconst val: string | number = \"hello\";\nif (isNumber(val)) {\n    const n: number = val; // TypeScript says number, but it's actually \"hello\"\n    n.toFixed(2); // Runtime error\n}\n```\n\n**Pattern 2: Assertion Functions (`asserts value is T`) Are Unchecked**\nSimilar to user-defined type guards, `asserts value is T` functions can have incorrect implementations.\n\n```typescript\nfunction assertIsString(x: unknown): asserts x is string {\n    // No check at all - always passes\n}\n\nconst value: unknown = 42;\nassertIsString(value);\nconst len: number = value.length; // TypeScript thinks value is string, but it's 42\n```\n\n**Pattern 3: Aliased Narrowing Unsoundness with Mutable Objects**\nWhen a discriminant check is stored in a const, TypeScript tracks this (f20-f33 in controlFlowAliasing.ts). 89. str_replace_editor @ 2026-03-11T18:54:49.430505

Input

{
  "command": "create",
  "file_text": "# Security Review: TypeScript Compiler Type-Narrowing Implementation\n\n**Repository:** `github.com/sg-evals/TypeScript--v5.7.2`  \n**Commit:** `b962c094988bec036174da8372b1be9159bfbb12`  \n**Primary files reviewed:**\n- `src/compiler/checker.ts` (~26,600 lines) \u2014 type-checking and narrowing engine\n- `src/compiler/binder.ts` \u2014 control flow graph construction\n- `src/compiler/types.ts` \u2014 core type and flow-node data structures\n- `tests/cases/compiler/inferTypePredicates.ts`\n- `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/controlFlowAliasing.ts`\n- `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/assertionTypePredicates1.ts`\n- `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/controlFlowTypeofObject.ts`\n\n---\n\n## 1. Narrowing Architecture Overview\n\nTypeScript's type-narrowing system spans two major compiler phases: **binder** (control-flow graph construction) and **checker** (flow-type resolution).\n\n### 1.1 Control Flow Graph (binder.ts)\n\nThe binder walks the AST and records every point that can change what type a variable holds, creating a linked list of `FlowNode` objects. These are defined in `src/compiler/types.ts` (lines 4152\u20134242) as a discriminated union:\n\n```\nFlowNode =\n  | FlowUnreachable   \u2014 dead code after throw/return/break\n  | FlowStart         \u2014 function/file entry point\n  | FlowLabel         \u2014 join point (loop back-edge, if-else merge)\n  | FlowAssignment    \u2014 any assignment to a narrowable reference\n  | FlowCondition     \u2014 truthy/falsy branch of a conditional\n  | FlowSwitchClause  \u2014 individual case in a switch statement\n  | FlowArrayMutation \u2014 x.push(v), x[n] = v (widening side-effect)\n  | FlowCall          \u2014 call expression (may invoke an assertion function)\n  | FlowReduceLabel   \u2014 loop-analysis helper (antecedent merging)\n```\n\nEach node carries a `flags: FlowFlags` bitmask (e.g. `TrueCondition`, `FalseCondition`, `Assignment`, `Call`) and an `antecedent` back-pointer forming the execution chain.\n\n### 1.2 Flow-Type Resolution (checker.ts)\n\nAt use-sites, `getFlowTypeOfReference` walks the `FlowNode` chain backward from the current program point to determine the narrowed type. It dispatches to sub-functions for each node kind:\n\n| Flow node kind | Checker function | Narrowing mechanism |\n|---|---|---|\n| `FlowCondition` | `narrowTypeByCondition` \u2192 `narrowType` | Binary / logical / `typeof` / `instanceof` / `in` / equality |\n| `FlowCall` | `narrowTypeByCall` | Assertion function `asserts param is T` |\n| `FlowSwitchClause` | `narrowTypeBySwitchOnDiscriminant` | Literal/enum exhaustiveness |\n| `FlowAssignment` | `getTypeAtFlowAssignment` | Widen to declared type |\n| `FlowLabel` | Union of antecedent types (join) | Loop fixpoint / `IncompleteType` |\n| `FlowArrayMutation` | Widen array element type | `x.push()` invalidation |\n\nSub-dispatchers handle specific syntactic forms:\n\n- **`narrowTypeByTypeof`** \u2014 maps `typeof x === 'string'` to primitive subtypes  \n- **`narrowTypeByTypePredicate`** \u2014 applies user-declared `x is T` guards  \n- **`narrowTypeByInstanceof`** \u2014 prototype-chain subtype reduction  \n- **`narrowTypeByIn`** \u2014 property-presence narrowing  \n- **`narrowTypeByEquality`** \u2014 literal / discriminant equality  \n- **`narrowTypeByOptionalChain`** \u2014 non-nullish narrowing across `?.` chains  \n- **`getFlowTypeOfDestructuring`** (line ~11068) \u2014 destructuring-binding flow type  \n- **`getFlowTypeInConstructor`** (line ~11237) \u2014 constructor `this` narrowing  \n\n### 1.3 Aliased Narrowing (TS 5.x)\n\nTypeScript 5.x introduced *aliased narrowing*: when a `const` variable is initialised to a type-test expression (`const isCircle = shape.kind === 'circle'`) the compiler tracks the test through the alias so that `if (isCircle)` narrows `shape`.  \nTracking is disabled for `let` aliases (the compiler cannot prove the alias was not reassigned between the test and the use) and for mutable object properties.\n\n### 1.4 Inferred Type Predicates (TS 5.5+)\n\nStarting with TypeScript 5.5, the compiler optionally *infers* a type-predicate return type for functions whose body is a single discriminating expression. This is gated by the function having no explicit return-type annotation. If an explicit `x is T` annotation is present, the body is **not re-checked** against that claim.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Unsound Narrowing Patterns\n\nThe following patterns are reproducible type-safety holes. All produce no TypeScript diagnostic yet can yield incorrect runtime behaviour.\n\n---\n\n### Pattern 1 \u2014 Unchecked User-Defined Type Guards\n\n**Severity: CRITICAL**  \n**Location:** `src/compiler/types.ts:5620\u20135660` (`TypePredicateKind` enum, `IdentifierTypePredicate`), `src/compiler/checker.ts` (`narrowTypeByTypePredicate`)\n\n#### Description\n\nTypeScript unconditionally trusts the return-type annotation `x is T` on a function. The checker records the predicate in a `TypePredicate` object with `kind = TypePredicateKind.Identifier` and applies it during `narrowTypeByTypePredicate`. **The function body is never validated against the claimed type narrowing.** A deliberately or accidentally wrong guard silently corrupts the type of the narrowed variable for all callers.\n\nThis is an intentional design decision (functions are sometimes declared in external `.d.ts` files with no body at all), but it means every user-defined type guard is an unchecked contract.\n\n#### Proof-of-concept\n\n```typescript\n// UNSOUND: guard annotation disagrees with implementation\nfunction isNumber(x: string | number): x is number {\n    // Body tests for string, but annotation claims the result is number.\n    // TypeScript never compares the two.\n    return typeof x === 'string';\n}\n\ndeclare const value: string | number;\n\nif (isNumber(value)) {\n    // TypeScript: value is narrowed to `number`\n    // Runtime:    value is actually a string\n    const fixed = value.toFixed(2); // Runtime TypeError: value.toFixed is not a function\n}\n```\n\nThe same pattern, confirmed by `tests/cases/compiler/inferTypePredicates.ts` (lines 95\u201398):\n\n```typescript\n// Defer to explicit type guards, even when they're incorrect.\nfunction backwardsGuard(x: number | string): x is number {\n    return typeof x === 'string'; // body says string; annotation says number\n}\n```\n\nThe test file comment acknowledges that TypeScript defers unconditionally, treating incorrect guards as a user error rather than a type-system violation.\n\n#### Impact\n\n- Any consumer of a type-guarded function receives a value that TypeScript presents as type-safe but may crash on use.\n- In library code or `.d.ts` files, incorrect guards are particularly dangerous because the violation is invisible to downstream authors.\n- Enables deliberate type smuggling: an attacker who can introduce or modify a type-guard function can cause a specific type to flow into a branch where the compiler asserts a different, incompatible type.\n\n#### Recommendation\n\n1. For inferred predicates (no explicit annotation), TypeScript 5.5+ already verifies the body, so no change is needed there.  \n2. For explicit annotations, consider adding an optional `--strictTypePredicates` mode that warns when the checked type of the true-branch return differs structurally from the declared predicate type.  \n3. Code-review guidance: treat every `x is T` annotation as a manual `as` cast. Require that guard functions have dedicated unit tests that exercise both the true and false branches.\n\n---\n\n### Pattern 2 \u2014 Unchecked Assertion Functions (`asserts param is T`)\n\n**Severity: HIGH**  \n**Location:** `src/compiler/types.ts:5653\u20135658` (`AssertsIdentifierTypePredicate`), `src/compiler/checker.ts` (`narrowTypeByCall` via `FlowCall` nodes)\n\n#### Description\n\n`asserts param is T` functions represent a more aggressive form of the same problem: when such a function returns normally, the checker *permanently* narrows the argument's type for the remainder of the calling scope (unlike a type guard, where the narrowing is conditional). The narrowing is applied by visiting `FlowCall` nodes in `getFlowTypeOfReference`. Again, **the body is never verified**.\n\nTwo additional sub-cases:\n\n**2a \u2014 Empty body (no check at all):**\n\n```typescript\nfunction assertIsString(x: unknown): asserts x is string {\n    // body intentionally left empty\n}\n\nconst value: unknown = 42;\nassertIsString(value); // TypeScript: value is now `string` in the enclosing scope\nconsole.log(value.length); // TypeScript: OK. Runtime: undefined (number has no .length)\n```\n\n**2b \u2014 Incorrect check:**\n\n```typescript\nfunction assertNonNull<T>(x: T): asserts x is NonNullable<T> {\n    // Incorrect: only checks for undefined, not null\n    if (x === undefined) throw new Error('undefined');\n    // null passes silently\n}\n\nconst maybeNull: string | null = null;\nassertNonNull(maybeNull);  // does not throw \u2014 null passes the check\nconst len = maybeNull.length; // TypeScript thinks string; Runtime: TypeError: Cannot read length of null\n```\n\n(Confirmed by `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/assertionTypePredicates1.ts` lines 10\u201312.)\n\n#### Impact\n\n- Assertion functions that narrow `unknown` or `any` to a specific type with no runtime check can let arbitrary values masquerade as typed objects, defeating all subsequent type-driven decisions.\n- Widely used in test harnesses and framework initialisation code, where incorrect assertions produce subtle test-passing-but-production-crashing bugs.\n\n#### Recommendation\n\n1. In strict mode, emit a warning when an `asserts x is T` function body does not contain a conditional that rejects values not assignable to `T`.  \n2. Promote the use of `zod`, `io-ts`, or similar runtime validation libraries rather than hand-written assertion functions for values crossing trust boundaries (e.g. network I/O, `JSON.parse`).  \n3. Lint rule: flag any `asserts x is T` whose body is shorter than one `if` statement.\n\n---\n\n### Pattern 3 \u2014 `typeof x === 'object'` Does Not Exclude `null`\n\n**Severity: MEDIUM**  \n**Location:** `src/compiler/checker.ts` (`narrowTypeByTypeof`); `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/controlFlowTypeofObject.ts`\n\n#### Description\n\nIn JavaScript, `typeof null === 'object'` evaluates to `true`. TypeScript's `narrowTypeByTypeof` correctly models this: after `if (typeof x === 'object')`, `null` is **not** excluded from the narrowed type. Code that trusts the narrowed type to be non-null without an additional check can crash.\n\n```typescript\nfunction processObject(x: string | number | object | null): void {\n    if (typeof x === 'object') {\n        // TypeScript narrows x to: object | null\n        // But developers commonly expect: object (non-null)\n        console.log(x.toString()); // TypeScript: OK (object has toString)\n                                   // Runtime: TypeError if x === null\n    }\n}\n\nprocessObject(null); // crashes at runtime, no compile-time error\n```\n\nThe test file `controlFlowTypeofObject.ts` exercises this behaviour \u2014 the test confirms that `typeof x === 'object'` followed by an `obj(x)` call (where `obj` requires `object`) still passes type-checking even when `x` is `unknown` (which includes `null`).\n\nA subtler variant arises when `null` is excluded from the declared type by `strictNullChecks` but the runtime receives a value from a `JSON.parse` or a weakly-typed API:\n\n```typescript\n// With strictNullChecks, x is declared as object (not object | null)\nfunction dangerous(x: object): string {\n    if (typeof x === 'object') {\n        // narrowed to `object` \u2014 x cannot be null per the declaration\n        return (x as any).name; // still crashes if caller passes null via `any` escape\n    }\n    return '';\n}\n```\n\n#### Impact\n\n- Any code that checks `typeof x === 'object'` and then dereferences a property without a subsequent null check is vulnerable to a `TypeError` from `null`.\n- This is a known JavaScript footgun but TypeScript's narrowing does not add an automatic guard, leaving the responsibility entirely with the developer.\n- The risk is elevated in generic utility functions, serialisation paths, and any code receiving data from external sources via `any`/`unknown`.\n\n#### Recommendation\n\n1. When narrowing by `typeof x === 'object'`, consider automatically intersecting the result type with `NonNullable<...>` only when the declared type already excludes `null` (i.e. in strict mode with no `| null` in the type). This is already partially done for some branches but not universally.  \n2. Publish a lint rule (e.g. via `eslint-plugin-typescript`) that flags `typeof x === 'object'` without an immediately following `&& x !== null` guard when operating on a type that includes `null`.  \n3. Document this behaviour prominently in the narrowing chapter of the TypeScript handbook.\n\n---\n\n### Pattern 4 \u2014 Aliased Narrowing Staleness in Closures and Async Contexts\n\n**Severity: MEDIUM**  \n**Location:** `src/compiler/checker.ts` (`narrowTypeByCondition` aliased-discriminant path); `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/controlFlowAliasing.ts`\n\n#### Description\n\nTypeScript 5.x aliased narrowing allows a `const` alias of a type-test result to narrow the source variable. The compiler conservatively refuses to apply the alias in loops and when the underlying variable is `let` or mutable. However, closures and asynchronous functions create a temporal gap between when the alias is captured and when it is consumed that the checker does not model.\n\n**4a \u2014 Captured alias used after `await`:**\n\n```typescript\nasync function process(event: { type: 'click' | 'keydown'; key?: string }) {\n    const isKey = event.type === 'keydown';\n    await fetch('/log'); // event.type may have changed if event is a shared reference\n    if (isKey) {\n        // TypeScript narrows event to { type: 'keydown'; key?: string }\n        // Runtime: event.type might now be 'click' if the event object was mutated\n        console.log(event.key?.toUpperCase());\n    }\n}\n```\n\n**4b \u2014 Destructured discriminant diverges from payload:**\n\nFrom `controlFlowAliasing.ts` (lines 255\u2013275), the TypeScript team explicitly acknowledged that destructuring the discriminant (`kind`) from a discriminated union into a separate binding breaks the link to the payload:\n\n```typescript\ntype Data = { kind: 'str'; payload: string } | { kind: 'num'; payload: number };\n\nfunction process({ kind, payload }: Data) {\n    if (kind === 'str') {\n        // TypeScript: payload is `string | number` \u2014 NOT narrowed!\n        // Because `kind` and `payload` are independent bindings after destructuring\n        const s: string = payload; // ERROR (correctly flagged)\n    }\n}\n```\n\nWhile TypeScript correctly refuses to narrow `payload` here, many developers are surprised by this gap and resort to a double cast (`payload as string`) \u2014 which is unsound.\n\n**4c \u2014 `const` alias for mutable property is not invalidated on mutation:**\n\n```typescript\nfunction f(outer: { readonly obj: { kind: 'foo'; foo: string } | { kind: 'bar'; bar: number } }) {\n    const isFoo = outer.obj.kind === 'foo'; // narrowed: outer.obj is readonly so OK\n    // BUT: outer itself is mutable (not readonly), and someone could rebind outer.obj\n    // via an aliased reference:\n    const ref = outer as { obj: { kind: 'foo'; foo: string } | { kind: 'bar'; bar: number } };\n    ref.obj = { kind: 'bar', bar: 99 };    // TypeScript allows: ref.obj is writable\n    if (isFoo) {\n        outer.obj.foo; // TypeScript: narrowed to foo-variant\n                       // Runtime: outer.obj is now bar-variant \u2192 undefined\n    }\n}\n```\n\n(Pattern derived from `controlFlowAliasing.ts` `f26`/`f27` pair: TypeScript tracks `readonly obj` but not mutations through aliased mutable views.)\n\n#### Impact\n\n- In async code, captured boolean aliases can silently become stale after `await` points if the narrowed object is shared state.  \n- Mutation through a separately-typed alias (stripping `readonly`) is not tracked by the flow graph and can make aliased narrowing silently wrong.\n\n#### Recommendation\n\n1. Emit a warning when a `FlowCondition` alias is used after an `await` point in an `async` function, since `await` is a potential mutation boundary for shared references.  \n2. Do not apply aliased narrowing when the aliased reference is a property of a non-`readonly` object, as TypeScript already does for directly mutable `let` variables \u2014 extend this conservatism to the aliased-property path.  \n3. Developer guidance: re-narrow inside closures/async callbacks rather than relying on pre-captured boolean aliases.\n\n---\n\n### Pattern 5 \u2014 `instanceof` Narrowing Does Not Track Prototype Mutation\n\n**Severity: MEDIUM**  \n**Location:** `src/compiler/checker.ts` (`narrowTypeByInstanceof`), binder `FlowCondition` nodes\n\n#### Description\n\n`instanceof` narrowing assumes that prototype chains are static. TypeScript narrows `x` to `C` after `x instanceof C`, mapping this to the class's instance type through the constructor's `prototype` property. This is sound under the JavaScript specification's class model, but JavaScript allows runtime prototype mutation:\n\n```typescript\nclass Animal {\n    speak() { return 'roar'; }\n}\nclass Dog extends Animal {\n    fetch() { return 'fetch!'; }\n}\n\ndeclare const pet: Animal;\nif (pet instanceof Dog) {\n    pet.fetch(); // TypeScript: safe. Runtime: safe, usually.\n}\n\n// But:\nObject.setPrototypeOf(Dog.prototype, null); // sever the chain\nconst notADog = new Dog();\nconsole.log(notADog instanceof Dog);       // true (own proto still Dog.prototype)\nconsole.log(notADog instanceof Animal);    // FALSE (chain severed)\n\n// TypeScript does not model setPrototypeOf.\n// After: if (x instanceof Animal) \u2014 x might not actually have Animal's methods.\n```\n\nMore practically relevant is the scenario with cross-realm objects (e.g. values from a different `vm.runInNewContext` or iframe `window`): `instanceof` is `false` even for objects that are structurally identical instances of the same class definition, so narrowing would not occur when it should. TypeScript cannot model realm boundaries at all.\n\n#### Impact\n\n- Security-critical code that relies on `instanceof` to gate access to privileged operations can be bypassed by supplying cross-realm objects.\n- Prototype-chain mutation, while rare, can produce objects whose `instanceof` results diverge from their actual structural properties, causing narrowed code to reach incorrect method calls.\n\n#### Recommendation\n\n1. Add a compiler warning for `Object.setPrototypeOf` calls in strict mode, or at minimum flag them with a lint rule.  \n2. For security-critical runtime checks, prefer structural validation (checking property presence/types) over `instanceof`, or use brand-checking (a private symbol/property that cannot be forged).  \n3. Document cross-realm `instanceof` limitations in the handbook.\n\n---\n\n### Pattern 6 \u2014 Inferred Type-Predicate False Positives via Conjunction\n\n**Severity: LOW\u2013MEDIUM**  \n**Location:** `src/compiler/checker.ts` (inferred-predicate logic, TS 5.5+); `tests/cases/compiler/inferTypePredicates.ts` (lines 112\u2013119)\n\n#### Description\n\nTypeScript 5.5+ infers a type predicate for a function that returns a simple discriminating expression. The inference is gated on the \"false\" branch: the function must narrow the type away in *both* the true and the false branch for the predicate to be inferred. However, when the return expression is a conjunction (`typeof x === 'string' && Math.random() > 0.5`), TypeScript correctly refuses to infer a predicate because the false branch is ambiguous. But the test file documents this nuance only in a comment \u2014 it is easy for developers to accidentally trip this with more subtle conjunctions.\n\nMore critically, the same test file shows that once an *explicit* type-predicate annotation is added, all bets are off (Pattern 1 above). The inferred-predicate path is safer precisely because it validates the body, but developers often add annotations to make the predicate explicit \u2014 breaking that safety.\n\n```typescript\n// SAFE: TypeScript 5.5+ infers the predicate and validates the body\nfunction isNonNull(x: number | null) {\n    return x !== null; // inferred: x is number (in true branch)\n}\n\n// UNSAFE: explicit annotation defeats body validation\nfunction isNonNull2(x: number | null): x is number {\n    return x !== null; // annotation taken at face value, body is NOT re-verified\n}\n\n// UNSOUND: wrong explicit annotation, accepted silently\nfunction isNonNull3(x: number | null): x is number {\n    return x === null; // always returns the opposite \u2014 TypeScript accepts this\n}\n```\n\n#### Impact\n\n- Lower severity than Pattern 1 because it only manifests when an explicit predicate annotation is added.\n- The gap between inferred (verified) and explicit (unverified) predicates is a footgun for developers migrating from inferred to annotated forms.\n\n#### Recommendation\n\n1. Add a `--strictTypePredicates` flag that extends the inferred-predicate body-validation logic to explicit predicates as well.  \n2. In the absence of such a flag, emit a suggestion-level diagnostic when an explicit predicate annotation is added to a function whose body would have produced a *different* inferred predicate.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Severity Summary\n\n| # | Pattern | Severity | Exploitability | Type-safety breach |\n|---|---|---|---|---|\n| 1 | Unchecked user-defined type guards | **CRITICAL** | Deliberate or accidental | Complete: arbitrary type forged |\n| 2 | Unchecked assertion functions | **HIGH** | Deliberate or accidental | Complete: permanent wrong narrowing |\n| 3 | `typeof x === 'object'` includes `null` | **MEDIUM** | Accidental | Partial: null deref crash |\n| 4 | Aliased narrowing staleness in closures/async | **MEDIUM** | Accidental | Partial: stale narrowing after mutation |\n| 5 | `instanceof` does not track prototype mutation | **MEDIUM** | Deliberate (cross-realm) | Partial: class-check bypass |\n| 6 | Explicit annotation defeats inferred-predicate validation | **LOW\u2013MEDIUM** | Accidental | Partial: incorrect narrowing |\n\n---\n\n## 4. Architectural Recommendations\n\n### 4.1 Short-term (compiler flags)\n\n1. **`--strictTypePredicates`** \u2014 When enabled, verify that the body of a function with an explicit `x is T` annotation is consistent with the claimed narrowing (i.e. the inferred type of the true-returning branch is assignable to `T`). Same for `asserts x is T`.  \n2. **Warn on `asserts` with empty bodies** \u2014 A function declared `asserts x is T` with no `if`/`throw` in its body is almost certainly a bug; emit a diagnostic even without `--strict`.  \n3. **`await`-boundary alias invalidation** \u2014 In an `async` function, invalidate aliased narrowing across `await` expressions for references that are not provably immutable (not `const` primitives or frozen objects).\n\n### 4.2 Medium-term (type-system improvements)\n\n4. **`typeof x === 'object'` \u2192 `object & NonNullable<...>`** \u2014 When the declared type already excludes `null`, the `typeof` narrowing should produce a type that TypeScript can statically prove non-null.  \n5. **Aliased narrowing scope limitation** \u2014 Do not propagate aliased narrowing into callbacks or arrow functions that may execute later (similar to how TypeScript already invalidates narrowings inside closures for mutable `let` variables).\n\n### 4.3 Long-term (soundness)\n\n6. **Opaque/brand types for security boundaries** \u2014 Provide first-class support for nominal/branded types so that security-critical code can use brand-checks rather than `instanceof` or user-defined type guards.  \n7. **Runtime-validation integration** \u2014 Explore integration with a runtime schema library as a first-class compiler concept so that `asserts x is T` can optionally be backed by a generated runtime check.\n\n---\n\n## 5. References\n\n| Source file | Lines | Relevance |\n|---|---|---|\n| `src/compiler/types.ts` | 4152\u20134262 | `FlowNode` discriminated union; `IncompleteType` |\n| `src/compiler/types.ts` | 5620\u20135660 | `TypePredicateKind`, `TypePredicate` variants |\n| `src/compiler/binder.ts` | imports | `FlowCondition`, `FlowCall`, `FlowAssignment`, `FlowSwitchClause`, `FlowArrayMutation`, `FlowReduceLabel` usage |\n| `src/compiler/checker.ts` | ~11068 | `getFlowTypeOfDestructuring` |\n| `src/compiler/checker.ts` | ~11237 | `getFlowTypeInConstructor` |\n| `tests/cases/compiler/inferTypePredicates.ts` | 95\u2013119 | Explicit guard body mismatch; flaky guard with `&&` |\n| `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/controlFlowAliasing.ts` | 1\u2013285 | Full aliased-narrowing test suite; acknowledged limitations |\n| `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/assertionTypePredicates1.ts` | 4\u2013114 | Assertion-function forms |\n| `tests/cases/conformance/controlFlow/controlFlowTypeofObject.ts` | 1\u201335 | `typeof === 'object'` test cases |\n",
